Explanation: In the text above, there was a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. In Imam's view, qisas is not obligatory, while in the Sahibayn's view, for the mawdihah qisas is obligatory and for both eyes diyah is obligatory. So here the proofs of both sides are presented. This is the proof of the Sahibayn. Its summary is that two parts are affected by the blow: the head and both eyes, and the multiplicity of the place of injury proves multiplicity, so two penalties are due: one qisas and the other diyah. Question: Since he did not intend to damage the eye, doubt arises and doubts remove qisas, so qisas should not be? Answer: We do not make qisas obligatory for the eyes, only for the mawdihah which has no doubt; the doubt in the eye injury does not affect the mawdihah. An example: Zayd shot Khalid with an arrow, and the arrow passed through him and hit Sajid, killing him. Then qisas is obligatory on Zayd for Khalid, and diyah for Sajid. In summary, the doubt in Sajid's killing did not create doubt in Khalid's killing; likewise here. The proof of Abu Hanifa: 'And he said: the first wound is transitive, and the penalty is in kind, and he has no power over the transitivity, so wealth is obligatory. Also because the act is one in reality, which is the movement that occurred, and similarly the place is united in some respect due to the connection of one to the other, so the end gave rise to the doubt of error in the beginning. Unlike two persons, because one of them is not from the transitivity of the other. And unlike when the knife falls on the finger, because it is not a deliberate act.' Translation: And Abu Hanifa's proof is that the first wound is transitive, and the penalty is in kind, and the transitivity is not within his power, so wealth is obligatory. Also because the act is really one, which is the movement that existed, and similarly the place is united due to the connection of one to the other; so the end of the act created doubt of error in the beginning. Unlike two persons, because one of them is not from the transitivity of the other. And unlike when a knife falls on a finger, because it is not an intended act. Explanation: This is Imam Abu Hanifa's proof that qisas is not obligatory in the first nor in the second, but in the second there is diyah and in the first there is arsh. Because qisas requires equality, and where equality is impossible, qisas does not occur. In this case, the first wound is transitive, so it would be necessary for the second wound to also be transitive, but making the wound transitive or not is not within human capacity. So qisas becomes impossible, and wealth (diyah) becomes obligatory. Also, the act of the striker is one, as is clear. And due to the strong connection between the two places, they are considered united. That is, the head and eye are strongly connected, so the place is one. And there is doubt at the end of the act and place, so the doubt at the end creates doubt in the beginning of the act and place, and doubt removes qisas, so qisas is dropped. Then if it is said: for the case where one bullet kills two, say likewise that qisas is not obligatory in the first? This is an analogy with a difference; in that case the discussion is about transitivity from a single bullet, neither the place is one nor is there transitivity from one to the other; the death of the second is not due to transitivity from the first but due to being hit by the arrow. If Zayd cut off Khalid's finger, then due to the tremor of Zayd's hand, a knife fell on another finger, would you say that as qisas is not obligatory in the second, so also not in the first? This too is an analogy with a difference because here the second act is not the effect of the first so that the second could be considered a completion of the first; thus no doubt arises in the first, because these two acts are separate. In the present case, the second is due to the transitivity of the first, so the second can be considered a completion of the first.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute over a case where a person strikes another on the head, causing a mawdihah (a wound that exposes the bone), and as a result the victim loses his eyesight. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that two separate penalties are due: qisas for the intentional wound (mawdihah) and diyah (blood money) for the loss of eyesight. Their reasoning is that two distinct loci of injury exist — the head and the eyes — and the multiplicity of the locus establishes multiplicity of penalties. They address the objection that the striker did not intend to blind the victim; they respond that the doubt in the eye injury does not affect the mawdihah, which is a deliberate wound. They analogize to a single arrow that passes through and kills two persons: qisas for the first, diyah for the second. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that only one set of compensation is due: arsh (compensation) for the wound and diyah for the eye, but no qisas at all. His proof is that the first wound is transitive (sariyah), meaning its effect spreads to another part. The penalty for an intentional wound is qisas, but qisas requires equivalence in kind, and the transitivity of the wound is not under the striker's control. Thus qisas is impossible, so only financial compensation is due. Also, the act is really one (a single blow), and the two places (head and eye) are deeply connected, so they are considered one locus. The doubt at the end (the unintended eye injury) creates doubt in the beginning (the whole act), and doubt prevents qisas. He distinguishes the arrow example: there the two deaths are not connected by transitivity (the arrow kills separately), so qisas for the first and diyah for the second is correct. Similarly, if a knife falls on a finger after cutting another, those are separate acts. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the view of Abu Hanifa according to the Hanafi school, as qisas is suspended due to doubt.

Key Terms
موضحہ (mawdihah): A wound on the head that exposes the bone.
ساری (sārī): Transitive; a wound whose effect spreads to another part of the body.
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliation in kind for intentional bodily harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is qisas not obligatory in the case of a head wound that causes blindness?
A) Because the wound was accidental.
B) Because the wound is transitive and exact equivalence is impossible.
C) Because the victim forgave the perpetrator.
D) Because the eye is not a limb subject to qisas.

Key Takeaway
In a mawdihah that causes blindness, Abu Hanifa's view (mufta bihi) holds that transitivity blocks qisas, requiring only financial compensation.

Cutting One Finger Causing Paralysis of Adjacent Finger: Qisas Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas for paralysis from injury to adjacent finger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on qisas when an intentional cut to one finger causes paralysis of an adjacent finger, and apply the principle that transitivity (sirāyah) affects qisas only when the secondary injury is also qisas-eligible.

Core Idea
Transitivity of an intentional injury blocks qisas only if the secondary injury is also subject to qisas.

When an intentional injury unintentionally harms another body part, does qisas still apply? The Hanafi school debated this question using a case where a finger cut leads to paralysis of an adjacent finger. Imam Abu Hanifa held that transitivity creates doubt and drops qisas entirely, while the Sahibayn, Zufar, and Hasan distinguished between direct and indirect effects. This lesson unpacks the disagreement and the key principle from Imam Muhammad's narration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the dispute
The case: a person cuts off one finger of another, and the adjacent finger becomes paralyzed (without being cut). The dispute is whether qisas is required for the cut finger and/or the paralysis.
Paralysis (shalal) is not a cut; it's loss of function.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Imam Abu Hanifa: No qisas for either injury; only financial compensation. His argument: the injury to the first finger transits to the second, creating doubt about whether the paralysis is directly caused, and qisas is dropped in cases of doubt.
Same reasoning as in the earlier head wound case (mawdihah causing blindness).
3
State the position of the Sahibayn, Zufar, and Hasan
They hold: qisas for the cut finger (direct and intentional), and arsh (fixed compensation) for the paralyzed finger (indirect, not qisas-eligible).
They consider the first act as a separate, direct injury not nullified by the secondary effect.
4
Trace the principle from Imam Muhammad's narration
According to a narration from Ibn Sam'ah, Imam Muhammad stated: when a wound that requires qisas (e.g., mawdihah) transits to another part that also permits qisas (e.g., eye), qisas is obligatory for the original wound. But if the secondary injury is not qisas-eligible (like paralysis), qisas does not extend to it.
This principle supports the view of the Sahibayn: since paralysis is not qisas-eligible, qisas remains only for the cut finger.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, offender pays diyah/arsh for both injuries (no qisas). Under the others, offender loses his finger via qisas plus pays arsh for the paralysis.
The majority position (Sahibayn, Zufar, Hasan) is consistent with Imam Muhammad's principle and may be considered the stronger view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قطع اصبعا فشلت الى جنبها اخرى فلا قصاص فى شئ من ذالك عند ابي حنيفة وقالا وزفر حسن يقتص من الأولى وفى الثانية ارشها والوجه من الجانبين قد ذكرناه قدوری نے فرمایا اور اگر ایک انگلی کاٹ دی پس اس کے پہلو میں دوسری انگلی شل ہوگئی تو ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ان دونوں میں ی میں قصاص نہیں ہے اور صاحبین اور زفر اور حسن نے فرمایا کہ اول کا قصاص لیا جائے گا۔ اور دوسری میں اس کا ارش ہو گا اور جانبین لیل وہی ہے جس کو ہم ذکر کر چکے ہیں۔ بح ایک انگلی کاٹی ، برابر میں دوسری بھی شل ہوگئی تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک قصاص نہ ہوگا، اور مذکورہ چار حضرات کے نزدیک کا قصاص اور ثانی کی دبیت واجب ہوگی۔ یہاں بھی فریقین کی دلیلیں وہی ہیں جو اد پر والے مسئلہ میں تھیں ابن سماع کی روایت کے مطابق امام محمد کے نزدیک قصاص کے بارے میں قاعدہ کلیہ ی ابن سماعة عن محمد فى المسألة الأولى وهو ما اذا شج موضحة فذهب بصره انه يجب القصاص ما لان الحاصل بالسراية مباشرة كما في النفس والبصر يجرى فيه القصاص بخلاف الخلافية الاخيرة الشلل لا قصاص فيه فصار الاصل عند محمد على هذه الرواية ان سراية ما يجب فيه القصاص الى ما كن فيه القصاص يوجب الاقتصاص كما لوالت الى النفس وقد وقع الأول ظلما
And if he cut off a finger and the adjacent finger became paralyzed, then according to Abu Hanifa there is no qisas in any of that. And the Sahibayn, Zufar, and Hasan said: qisas will be taken from the first, and in the second its arsh (compensation). And the evidence on both sides we have already mentioned. Quduri said: And if he cut one finger, then the adjacent finger became paralyzed, then according to Abu Hanifa there is no qisas in either. And the four mentioned say: qisas for the first and diyah for the second. The proofs are the same as in the previous issue. According to the narration of Ibn Sam'ah from Muhammad concerning the first issue: when he struck a mawdihah and the eyesight was lost, that qisas is obligatory because what is obtained by transitivity is like direct action, as in the case of a person, and eyesight is subject to qisas. Unlike the last issue where paralysis has no qisas. So the established principle according to Muhammad in this narration is that transitivity of what requires qisas into something that also requires qisas necessitates qisas, just as if it extended to a person. And the first act was unjust.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute on a variant case: a person cuts one finger of another, and due to the injury, the adjacent finger becomes paralyzed (without being cut). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that no qisas is due for either finger — neither for the cut finger nor the paralyzed one. He applies the same reasoning as in the previous case: the injury to the first finger is transitive, causing the paralysis, so qisas is impossible due to doubt. The Sahibayn, Zufar, and Hasan (other Hanafi scholars) hold that qisas is applicable to the first finger (the intentional cut), and only arsh (compensation) is due for the paralyzed finger. They argue that the first act is direct and deliberate, so qisas is not nullified by the subsequent effect. The text then mentions a narration from Ibn Sam'ah from Imam Muhammad on the earlier issue (mawdihah causing blindness), stating a general principle: when a wound that requires qisas (like a deliberate mawdihah) extends by transitivity to another part that also permits qisas (like the eye), then qisas is obligatory for the original wound because the effect is considered direct. But in the case of paralysis, qisas is not applicable because paralysis is not subject to qisas. This establishes a differentiating criterion: the ruling on transitivity depends on whether the secondary injury is itself subject to qisas or not.

Key Terms
شلل (shalal): Paralysis; loss of function of a limb without severance.
إرش (arsh): Fixed compensation for injuries that do not have a prescribed diyah amount.
سرعة (sirāyah): Transitivity; the spread of an injury's effect to another part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration from Imam Muhammad (via Ibn Sam'ah), when does transitivity of an injury obligate qisas for the original wound?
A) When the secondary injury is also subject to qisas.
B) Always, regardless of the secondary injury.
C) Only when the secondary injury is fatal.
D) Never, because transitivity always creates doubt.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on transitivity (sirāyah) depends on whether the secondary injury is itself subject to qisas; if it is not, qisas is limited to the original intentional injury.

Qisas for Mawdihah Causing Blindness – The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas for head wound leading to blindness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a shajjah mawdihah that causes loss of sight, state the mashhoor (well-known) position and its proof, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Core Idea
When a head wound causes blindness, direct causation (mubasharah) gives qisas, but indirect causation (tasbib) gives only diyat.

This lesson explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. Both views are transmitted from Imam Muhammad – the mashhoor view, shared with Abu Yusuf, and a variant narrated by Ibn Sama'ah. The core issue is whether the principle of saraayah (transference) applies to a wound that directly admits qisas when it extends to another body part that also admits qisas. Understanding the distinction between direct and indirect causation is key to grasping the Hanafi ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi mashhoor position
Identify the well-known view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad in the mashhoor narration): qisas for the wound (shajjah), diyat for the loss of sight.
2
Identify the variant view from Ibn Sama'ah
Recognize the second narration from Imam Muhammad: qisas for both the wound and the sight.
3
Analyze the proof for the mashhoor view
Understand the argument that loss of sight is tasbib (indirect causation) and qisas does not apply to tasbib. The wound remains independently liable for qisas.
4
Analyze the proof for Ibn Sama'ah's narration
Follow the reasoning that the second injury is direct via saraayah (transference) and that the eye admits qisas, analogous to a wound causing death where the original wound is annulled.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and thamarah al-khilaf
Determine which view is mufta bihi and explain the practical consequence: under mashhoor, the perpetrator receives qisas on the head and pays diyat for sight; under the variant, he also loses his eye.

Translation: And Ibn Sama'ah narrated in the first issue: when he inflicts a shajjah mawdihah and then his sight is lost, then in both qisas is wajib because what is obtained through saraayah is direct (mubasharah) like in the case of the self, and sight is a thing in which qisas is carried out, unlike the last disputed issue because paralysis does not have qisas. So according to this narration, near Muhammad the general principle is that the saraayah of a wound that has qisas to a wound that admits qisas makes qisas wajib, like the wound saraayah to the self, even if the first was unjust. Explanation: At the beginning of this page of Hidayah a disputed issue passed in which according to the well-known view Imam Muhammad is with Imam Abu Yusuf, and they made qisas for the shajjah mawdihah and diyat for the eyes. In this issue Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Imam Muhammad a second narration that qisas will be taken in both. That is, in the eye and in the mawdihah, because a person does some actions intentionally and some by production, and when the second act occurred through saraayah of the first, then like the first, the second is also considered direct. For example, if he inflicted a wound and the wounded died due to that wound, qisas is wajib, and the eye is a thing in which qisas is carried out, so here qisas is made wajib in both. However, in the text of this issue it was said that near the Sahibayn qisas for the first and arsh for the second are wajib. The reason is that paralysis does not have qisas, so here arsh is made wajib. According to Ibn Sama'ah's narration, near Imam Muhammad the general principle is that when saraayah occurs from a wound that has qisas to a wound in which qisas can be taken, then qisas is wajib. For example, if he cut a finger and the person died from it, and the finger was unintentional, then qisas is wajib. The proof of the well-known view: And the face of the well-known is that the loss of sight is by way of tasbib. Do you not see that the shajjah remained causing qisas in itself, and there is no qisas in tasbib, unlike saraayah to the self because the first does not remain, so the second becomes direct. Translation: And the reason for the well-known saying is that the loss of sight is by way of tasbib. Do you not see that the shajjah itself is a cause of qisas, and there is no qisas in tasbib, unlike saraayah to the self because the first wound will not remain, so the second changes to direct. Explanation: In the previous, the proof of Imam Muhammad's narration narrated from Ibn Sama'ah was mentioned, and this is the proof of the well-known saying. The well-known saying was that qisas for the first and diyat for the second are wajib. Why? Because the loss of sight is by way of production, not by way of directness, and the principle is that in less than the self, qisas is not carried out on a cause, but only diyat is wajib, because qisas for the shajjah has already become wajib. Question: If someone fractured someone's head and due to the trauma of the wound he died, why is qisas wajib here, while here also there is a cause and qisas is not carried out on a cause? Answer: In the case of the death of the self, the first wound and its qisas do not remain, so it is considered non-existent, and only the death of the self is considered by way of directness, therefore qisas of the self is wajib.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a shajjah mawdihah (head wound exposing the bone) that causes loss of sight. The well-known (mashhoor) view, attributed to both Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf, holds that qisas (retribution) is required for the wound itself, while diyat (blood money) is required for the loss of sight. However, Ibn Sama'ah narrates from Imam Muhammad that qisas is required for both the wound and the sight. The commentary explains the reasoning behind each view. The mashhoor view argues that the loss of sight is caused by tasbib (indirect causation), and qisas does not apply to tasbib; the wound remains independently liable for qisas. In contrast, Ibn Sama'ah's narration applies the principle of saraayah (transference): when a wound that mandates qisas extends to another body part that also admits qisas, then qisas becomes due for both, similar to a wound that causes death where the initial wound is annulled and only death remains. The commentary further addresses an objection regarding the case of death, explaining that in death, the first wound is effectively nullified, so the death is considered direct (mubasharah), but in the case of a limb, the first wound remains, making the second indirect.

Key Terms
شجہ موضحہ (shajjah mawdihah): A head wound that exposes the bone.
سرایت (saraayah): The extension of a wound's effect to another body part, affecting liability.
تسبیب (tasbib): Indirect causation; qisas is not applicable to tasbib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mashhoor (well-known) view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a shajjah mawdihah that causes loss of sight?
A) Qisas is required for both the wound and the sight.
B) Qisas is required for the wound, and diyat is required for the sight.
C) Diyat is required for both.
D) No liability because the wound was tasbib.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between direct causation (mubasharah) for qisas and indirect causation (tasbib) for diyat; a wound that causes blindness is treated as direct for the wound and indirect for the blindness.

Ruling on Breaking Part of a Tooth and Two Mawdihah Wounds Merging
Intra-Hanafi dispute on saraayah of wounds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mashhoor Hanafi position and the opposing narration of Ibn Sama'ah regarding qisas for injuries that escalate, and identify the underlying principle of tasbib vs saraayah.

Core Idea
Indirect causation (tasbib) blocks qisas; only direct causation imposes it.

In Hanafi fiqh, the line between direct and indirect injury determines qisas. This lesson examines two scenarios from Kitab al-Diyat: breaking part of a tooth causing complete loss, and two mawdihah wounds merging. Both reveal a critical intra-Hanafi dispute between the well-known view and Ibn Sama'ah's narration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and resulting injury
For the tooth: breaking part causes the whole tooth to fall. For the wounds: two mawdihah become one.
2
State the mashhoor view and its basis
Mashhoor: no qisas because the greater injury is indirect (tasbib).
3
State Ibn Sama'ah's view and its basis
Ibn Sama'ah: qisas applies based on the saraayah principle - the injury spreads.
4
Compare the two principles
Tasbib blocks qisas by distinguishing direct from indirect; saraayah extends liability to all consequences.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical difference
The mashhoor is the relied-upon view: no qisas for the escalated injury.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دانت کا کچھ حصہ توڑ دیا جس سے سارا دانت گر گیا تو قصاص نہ ہوگا مگر ابن سماعہ کی روایت کے مطابق قصاص ہوگا۔ اور اگر دو موضحہ زخم لگائے پس وہ دونوں سڑ کر ایک ہو گئے تو یہ انہیں دو روایتوں پر ہے۔
He said: And if he broke part of a tooth and it fell, then no qisas except according to Ibn Sama'ah's narration. And if he inflicted two mawdihah wounds and they festered and became one, then it is according to these two narrations.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents two related rulings from Kitab al-Diyat. The first ruling: if someone breaks part of a tooth and the entire tooth falls out, according to the well-known view (mashhoor), there is no qisas because the act (breaking part) was direct, but the complete loss is indirect (tasbib). However, according to Ibn Sama'ah's narration from Imam Muhammad, qisas would apply based on the saraayah principle. The second ruling: if a person inflicts two separate mawdihah wounds (head wounds exposing bone) and they merge into one (e.g., due to infection), the ruling again depends on the two narrations: the mashhoor says no additional qisas, while Ibn Sama'ah's narration requires qisas. This shows that the dispute between the two narrations extends beyond the sight case to other injuries involving saraayah.

Key Terms
سن (sinn): Tooth.
موضحہ (mawdihah): A head wound exposing bone.
تسبيب (tasbib): Indirect causation; the act leads to harm through an intermediary.
سراية (saraayah): The principle that an injury spreads to adjoining parts, making the perpetrator liable for the full extent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No qisas is required.
C) Diyat is required from the perpetrator.
D) The tooth regrows, so no penalty.

Key Takeaway
The mashhoor Hanafi position on qisas for escalating injuries is based on the principle of tasbib: indirect causation does not incur qisas.

Dispute on Tooth Extraction and Regrowth: Arsh or Not?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether arsh is due when a tooth grows back

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on arsh when a tooth grows back and identify which position is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
If a tooth regrows after being knocked out, the Sahibayn say full arsh is due—Abu Hanifah says none, and Abu Yusuf sometimes says a just assessment.

This passage from Quduri presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when an adult's tooth is extracted and a new tooth grows naturally, does the perpetrator owe compensation? The ruling depends on whether the regrowth cancels the injury or is considered a separate blessing. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to handle cases where the consequence of a crime changes after the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the rulings of Abu Hanifah, the Sahibayn, and the additional narration from Abu Yusuf regarding compensation for a regrown tooth.
Abu Hanifah: no arsh. Sahibayn: full arsh. Abu Yusuf (alternate): hukumat al-adl.
2
Understand each imam's core argument
For each position, extract the reasoning: why does Abu Hanifah think arsh is dropped? Why do the Sahibayn insist on full arsh? Why does Abu Yusuf propose a just assessment?
Focus on the jinaayah (crime) and the new tooth as a 'new blessing'.
3
Trace the analogical reasoning (qiyas)
Notice that Abu Hanifah compares an adult's regrown tooth to a child's naturally growing tooth. Identify the asl (child), far' (adult), and illa (cause: loss of benefit/beauty undone by regrowth).
The Sahibayn reject this qiyas because the adult's extraction is a completed crime.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
Based on the school's preference, decide which view is the relied‑upon (mufta bihi) position today and state why.
The Sahibayn's view (full arsh) is the mufta bihi as it is the majority and Zahir al-Riwayah.

قال ولو قلع سن رجل فنبتت مكانها اخرى سقط الارش فى قول ابى حنيفة وقالا عليه الارش كاملا لان الجناية قد تحققت والحادث نعمة مبتدأة من الله تعالى وله ان الجناية انعدمت معنى فصار كما اذا قلع سن صبی فنبتت لا يجب الارش بالاجماع لانه لم يفت عليه منفعة ولازينة وعن أبي يوسف انه تجب حكومة عدل لمكان الالم الحاصل

سے جو حاصل ہوا۔ تشریح چھوٹے بچوں کے دانت گر کر دوسرے جمتے ہیں۔ لیکن بڑوں کے نہیں جمتے لہذا اگر کسی نے بچہ کا دانت توڑا، اور دوسرا دانت جم گیا تو چونکہ اسمیں نہ زینت ختم ہوئی اور نہ منفعت اسلئے اس میں بالا جماع ارش واجب نہ ہوگا لیکن امام ابو یوسف
کے نزدیک حکومت عدل واجب ہے، کیونکہ اس کے توڑنے
By mistake he extracted a tooth, in its place another tooth grew, is arsh wajib or not? Statements of the jurists. He said: And if he extracts a man's tooth and another grows in its place, the arsh falls according to Abu Hanifah's saying, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: full arsh is due because the injury has occurred, and the new growth is a new bounty from Allah. And his (Abu Hanifah's) argument is that the injury has ceased in effect, so it is like extracting a child's tooth which grows back: arsh is not due by consensus because no benefit or beauty was lost. And from Abu Yusuf it is transmitted that a just assessment (hukumat al-adl) is due because of the pain experienced. Translation: Quduri said: And if a man's tooth is extracted and in its place another grows, then in Abu Hanifah's saying arsh is dropped. And the Sahibayn said that full arsh is due because the crime has been established, and the thing that grows (new tooth) is a new blessing from Allah. And Abu Hanifah's proof is that the crime has become non-existent in effect, so it becomes like extracting a child's tooth which grows: by consensus arsh is not due because no benefit or beauty was lost. And from Abu Yusuf it is transmitted that a just assessment (hukumat al-adl) is due because of the pain that occurred. Explanation: Children's teeth fall out and others grow in their place. But adults' teeth do not grow. Therefore, if someone broke a child's tooth and another grew, since no beauty or benefit was lost, by consensus arsh is not due. But according to Imam Abu Yusuf, a just assessment is due because of the pain caused by breaking it.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage discusses a case where an adult's tooth is knocked out and a new tooth grows in its place naturally (which rarely happens in adults). The Hanafi imams differ on whether compensation (arsh) is required. Imam Abu Hanifah rules that no arsh is due because the injury has been effectively undone by Allah granting a new tooth; the situation is analogous to a child's tooth (where by consensus no arsh is due because the loss of benefit and beauty is temporary). Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that arsh is due because the criminal act (jinaayah) was completed, and the new tooth is an independent gift from Allah, not a restoration of the original. Imam Abu Yusuf also has a position that a just assessment (hukumat al-adl) is due to compensate for the pain suffered, even if the tooth regrows. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute where the ruling affects the amount of compensation.

Key Terms
ارش (arsh): Compensation for injury to a body part; determined by the Diyat schedule.
حکومت عدل (hukumat al-adl): A discretionary assessment by a fair judge of compensation for an injury not covered by fixed arsh.
جنایت (jinaayah): A criminal act causing injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the ruling if an adult's extracted tooth grows back?
A) No compensation is due.
B) Full arsh (compensation) is due.
C) Only a discretionary assessment (hukumat al-adl) is due.
D) The perpetrator is exempt because the tooth regrew.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi is that full arsh (compensation) is due when an adult's tooth is knocked out, even if a new tooth grows back.

Extracted Tooth Reinserted and Healed: Arsh Due Even After Healing
Ruling that arsh is due despite reattachment and healing, with exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that full arsh is due for a tooth extracted and reinserted that heals, and identify the exception when the original state in benefit and beauty is restored.

Core Idea
If a tooth is severed and reattaches, arsh is still due because the original state is irrecoverable.

Injuries that involve severing a body part raise the question: if the part is reattached and heals, is compensation still owed? This lesson presents the Hanafi position that the original state is never fully restored—the veins do not reconnect—so the full arsh remains obligatory. An important exception, cited from later authorities, waives arsh if the part returns exactly to its original function and appearance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is extracting another's tooth and the owner reinserting it, with flesh growing over it. The default ruling: the extractor owes full arsh.
The ruling applies to any body part, as shown by the ear example.
2
Understand the underlying reason (ʿilla)
Healing does not restore the original state because the severed veins (ʿuruq) do not reconnect. This is the basis for the obligation.
The ʿilla is the permanent loss of original state.
3
Note the exception from later authorities
Shaykh al-Islam (Durr Mukhtar) states: if the part returns to its original state in both benefit and beauty, then no arsh is due.
This exception is from a later relied-upon source, but the default remains the main ruling.
4
Apply the ruling to similar cases
The same ruling applies to an ear that is cut off and stuck back on; arsh is due unless original state is restored.
The ear example reinforces the general principle.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The mufta bihi is that arsh is due unless the exception condition is met. The burden of proving restoration is on the defendant.
In practice, full restoration is rarely established, so arsh is usually due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قلع سن غیره فردها صاحبها في مكانها ونبت عليه اللحم فعلى القالع الارش بكماله لان هذا مما يعتدبه اذ العروق لا تعود وكذا اذا قطع اذنه فالصقها فالتحمت لانها لا تعود الى ما كانت عليه قال شيخ الإسلام ان عادت الى حالتها الأولى فى المنفعته والجمال لا شئى عليه کمالو ثبت (درمختار)
And if someone extracts the tooth of another, and the owner puts it back in its place and flesh grows over it, then the extractor owes the full arsh because this regrowth is not considered valid, as the veins do not return. Similarly, if he cuts off his ear and he sticks it on and it heals, [arsh is due] because it will not return to its former state. Shaykh al-Islam said: if it returns to its original state in benefit and beauty, then nothing is due upon him, as is established (in Durr Mukhtar).
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding injuries to teeth and ears. The default ruling is that if a body part is severed and then reattached and heals, the arsh (compensation) is still due because the original state cannot be fully restored—the veins that were severed do not reconnect as before. The exception, quoted from Shaykh al-Islam (likely a reference to a later authority), is that if the part returns to its original state in both function and appearance, then no arsh is owed. This exception is found in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, a relied-upon late Hanafi work. The ruling underscores the Hanafi principle that bodily integrity is valued in its original state, and any deviation—even if healing occurs—does not negate the obligation of compensation.

Key Terms
ارش (arsh): Compensation for injury to a body part, assessed by value or predetermined amount.
عروق (uruq): Blood vessels or veins that are severed in an injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a tooth is extracted and then reinserted and heals, what is the ruling regarding arsh?
A) No arsh because it healed
B) Full arsh is due
C) Half arsh
D) Only if it does not heal

Key Takeaway
Full arsh is due for a severed tooth that reattaches and heals because the original state is irreversibly lost, unless perfect restoration in function and appearance is proven.

One-Year Waiting Period for Tooth Regrowth Before Qisas
Rationale for the one-year rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rationale for the one-year waiting period before qisas for tooth extraction and state the legal consequence if the tooth later regrows.

Core Idea
One year suffices for tooth regrowth; waiting indefinitely wastes rights.

In cases of intentional tooth extraction, the Hanafi school does not require waiting indefinitely for absolute certainty that the tooth will never regrow. Instead, it sets a practical one-year limit based on the usual growth period. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this ruling and the protective effect of doubt (shubha) if the tooth later regrows after qisas has been executed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default expectation
Note that the default would be to wait for ya's (certainty that the tooth will not regrow) before executing qisas. This is the baseline if no alternative were established.
Ya's means despair of regrowth, which may require waiting many years.
2
Understand the objection to indefinite waiting
The text argues that waiting for ya's would waste the victim's right to timely qisas. Identify the harmful consequence: the victim's right is unnecessarily delayed.
The principle of preventing harm (darar) is invoked.
3
Analyze the alternative: one-year presumption
The Hanafi school substitutes the indefinite wait with a one-year period based on the empirical observation that a tooth that will grow back typically does so within a year. This limits the delay and protects the right.
This is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom.
4
Examine the post-qisas scenario
If the tooth later grows after qisas, the execution is recognized as a mistake (it was not actually justified). However, qisas is not re-imposed because of the doubt (shubha) that was present at the time of execution. Instead, monetary compensation becomes obligatory.
Shubha here refers to the uncertainty about whether regrowth would occur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان ينبغي ان ينتظر الياس فى ذالك للقصاص الا ان فى اعتبار ذالك تضييع الحقوق فاكتفينا بالحول لانه تنبت فيه ظاهرا فاذا مضى الحول ولم تنبت قضينا بالقصاص واذا نبتت تبين انا اخطأنا فيه والاستيفاع كان بغير حق الا انه لا يجب القصاص للشبهة فيجب المال
And it would have been appropriate to wait for despair [of regrowth] in that matter for qisas, except that in considering that there is a waste of rights, so we sufficed with one year, because it usually grows in that time. So when the year passes and it has not grown, we decide qisas. And if it grows, it becomes clear that we erred in it and the exacting was without right, except that qisas is not obligatory due to the doubt, so property [compensation] is obligatory.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage provides the rationale for the one-year waiting period in cases of tooth extraction. Instead of waiting indefinitely for absolute certainty (ya's) that the tooth will not regrow, the Hanafi school sets a one-year limit because: (1) a tooth that will grow typically does so within a year, and (2) waiting longer would waste the victim's right to timely qisas. After one year, if the tooth has not grown, qisas is carried out. However, if after qisas the tooth later grows back, it becomes clear that the qisas was executed without right. But because there was a doubt (shubha) at the time of execution, qisas is not re-imposed; instead, the extractor must pay monetary compensation (500 dirhams) to the one who had his tooth extracted in retaliation.

Key Terms
الياس (ya's): State of despair or certainty that a tooth will not regrow.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt that prevents the execution of qisas after the fact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, why is a one-year waiting period prescribed instead of waiting for ya's (certainty of non-regrowth) before qisas for a tooth?
A) Because teeth always grow within one year.
B) To avoid wasting the victim's right to timely qisas.
C) Because the judge cannot wait longer by law.
D) Because the Quran commands one year.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school substitutes indefinite waiting for ya's with a one-year presumptive period to protect the victim's right to timely qisas, and if the tooth later regrows, doubt prevents reversal, but monetary compensation is due.

Loosened Tooth: Waiting Period and Evidentiary Rule
Ruling that for a loosened tooth, wait one year and accept victim's statement before year ends

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for a loosened tooth (wait one year) and apply the evidentiary rule (al-qawl lil-madrub) when the cause is disputed within the waiting period.

Core Idea
For a loosened tooth, wait one year; if it falls within the year, the victim's word is taken.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in the chapter of diyat (injuries) concerning a tooth loosened by a blow. The Hanafi school prescribes a one-year waiting period to determine the outcome. If during that year the tooth falls and the parties dispute the cause, the law favors the victim by accepting his statement, ensuring the delay serves its purpose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is striking a person's tooth causing it to become loose. The ruling is that the injurer must wait one year to see the effect of the blow.
2
Understand the purpose (ta'lil)
The delay is prescribed so that the effect of the act becomes apparent — whether the tooth will fall or stabilize naturally.
3
Apply the evidentiary rule
If the tooth falls before the year ends and the parties dispute the cause, the victim's statement is accepted (al-qawl lil-madrub).
This is a procedural protection for the victim.
4
Recognize the consequence
This rule ensures that the waiting period benefits the victim rather than the aggressor, i.e., the delay is beneficial (li-yakuna al-ta'jil mufidan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولو ضرب انسان سن انسان فتحرکت پستانی حولا ليظهر اثر فعله فلو اجله القاضى سنة ثم جاء المضروب وقد سقطت سنه فاختلفا قبل السنة فيما سقط بضربه فالقول للمضروب ليكون التاجيل مفيدا
He said: And if a man strikes the tooth of a man and it becomes loose, he waits one year so that the effect of his act becomes apparent. So if the judge gives him a one-year postponement, then the victim comes and his tooth has fallen, and they dispute before the year regarding whether it fell due to his blow, then the statement [over that] is of the victim, so that the delay is beneficial.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where a tooth is loosened but not yet extracted. The ruling is that a one-year waiting period is observed to determine whether the tooth will fall out or become stable. The judge grants this delay. If within the year the tooth falls out, and the parties dispute whether it was due to the blow or natural causes, the victim's claim is accepted (his word is taken) because the delay was meant to clarify the outcome; if the injury was indeed the cause, the victim should not be disadvantaged. This rule ensures that the purpose of the delay—to protect rights—is achieved.

Key Terms
تحركت (taharrakat): Became loose (of a tooth), not yet extracted.
القول للمضروب (al-qawl lil-madrub): The statement of the victim (injured party) is accepted in the dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The defendant's
B) The victim's
C) The judge's decision
D) Both parties' equally

Key Takeaway
For a loosened tooth, the Hanafi rule imposes a one-year waiting period and accepts the victim's claim as to causation within that year to ensure the delay is beneficial.

One-Year Wait for Tooth Regrowth and Consequences
Explains the one-year waiting period for a knocked-out tooth and legal outcomes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the one-year waiting period rule for a knocked-out tooth, explain its rationale based on istihsan, and apply the mufta bihi ruling distinguishing between children and adults.

Core Idea
A tooth's fate is decided after a year: if it regrows, no qisas; if not, qisas; but if later regrows, diyah due to doubt.

In this lesson, we examine a classic Hanafi ruling on retaliatory justice for a knocked-out tooth. The Hidayah presents a procedural waiting period and its legal consequences. Understanding this case reveals how Hanafi jurists balance strict qisas with practical concerns through istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the ruling
The passage addresses a tooth that is knocked out. The ruling prescribes a one-year waiting period to see if it regrows naturally. If it does, no qisas (retaliation) is taken. If it does not regrow after a year, qisas is taken.
2
Understand the proof (istihsan)
The proof is not a textual source but a rational consideration (istihsan): waiting indefinitely for 'absolute despair' would waste the victim's rights, so a practical one-year limit is set because a tooth typically regrows within that time. This is a juristic preference to avoid delay and protect rights.
The proof is based on maslaha (public interest) and practicality.
3
Note the consequence of error
If the tooth regrows after qisas has been taken, the qisas is considered an error. However, due to doubt (shubha), the qisas is not repeated. Instead, diyah (blood money, 500 dirhams) becomes obligatory.
This shows the Hanafi concern for certainty and fairness.
4
Distinguish between adults and children
The text prescribes a one-year wait, but the mufta bihi (operative opinion) in the Hanafi school is that adults do not need to wait; the year applies only to children because an adult's tooth is not expected to regrow.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf resolved by later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قصاص لینے میں ایک سال تک انتظار کی جائے اگر دانت جم آئے ورنہ قصاص لیا جائے گا

وكان ينبغي ان ينتظر الياس فى ذالك للقصاص الا ان فى اعتبار ذالك تضييع الحقوق فاكتفينا بالحول لانه تنبت فيه ظاهرا فاذا مضى الحول ولم تنبت قضينا بالقصاص واذا نبتت تبين انا اخطأنا فيه والاستيفاع كان بغير حق الا انه لا يجب القصاص للشبهة فيجب المال

ترجمہ اور مناسب تو یہ تھا کہ اس میں قصاص کے لئے نا امیدی کا انتظار کیا جاتا۔ مگر اس کا اعتبار کرنے میں حقوق کو ضائع کرنا تھا تو ہم نے ایک سال پر اکتفا کر لیا اس لئے کہ اس میں غالباً جم جاتا ہے پس جب کہ سال گزر گیا اور نہیں جما تو ہم نے قصاص کا فیصلہ کر دیا اور جب دانت جم گیا تو یہ بات واضح ہوگئی کہ اس میں ہم نے غلطی کی ہے اور قصاص لینا ناحق ہے مگر شبہ کی وجہ سے قصاص واجب نہ ہوگا تو مال واجب ہوگا۔ تشریح مصنف اسی پہلے مسئلہ پر گفتگو کرتے ہوئے فرماتے ہیں کہ ایک سال کی مہلت دینے کے بجائے مناسب تو یہ تھا قصاص لینے کے لئے اتنی مدت کا انتظار کیا جاتا کہ دانت جمنے سے بالکل نا امیدی ہو جاتی ۔
لیکن ایسا کرنے میں صحیح حقوق کا خطرہ تھا اس وجہ سے ہم نے صرف ایک سال کی مہلت کا فیصلہ کیا کیونکہ جب دانت کو اگنا ہوتا ہے تو وہ ایک سال میں جم جاتا ہے۔ لیکن جب سال گزر گیا اور دانت نہیں اگا تو ہم نے مجبور اقتصاص کا فیصلہ کر دیا۔ لیکن اگر اس کے بعد پھر دانت جم گیا تو اس سے ہمارے فیصلے کا غلط ہونا ظاہر ہو گیا اور یہ بھی ظاہر ہو گیا کہ قصاص ناحق لیا گیا ہے اس لئے پانچ سو درہم واجب کر دیئے گئے ۔
اور قاعدہ میں تو قصاص واجب ہونا چاہیے تھا لیکن شبہ کی وجہ سے قصاص ساقط ہو گیا اور جب قصاص ساقط ہوا تو مال واجب ہونا چاہیے۔
In taking qisas, one should wait up to a year; if the tooth regrows, fine; otherwise, qisas will be taken. And it would have been appropriate to wait for despair of regrowth for qisas, but considering that would waste rights, we sufficed with a year because it usually regrows within that time. So if the year passes and it has not regrown, we decree qisas; and if it regrows, it becomes clear that we erred and the qisas was taken unjustly, but due to the doubt, qisas is not obligatory, so diya is obligatory. The commentary explains that the author discusses this issue, stating that instead of giving a year's respite, it would have been appropriate to wait until utter despair of regrowth, but because that would risk rights, we decided on a one-year respite, since a tooth normally regrows within a year. If after the year it does not regrow, we reluctantly decree qisas. But if it later regrows, our decision is shown to be wrong, and it becomes clear that qisas was taken unjustly, so five hundred dirhams are made obligatory. As a rule, qisas should have been obligatory, but due to the doubt, qisas is dropped and diya becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah commentary. It addresses the case of a tooth that is knocked out. The ruling prescribes a one-year waiting period to see if the tooth regrows naturally. If it does, no qisas (retaliation) is taken; if it does not, qisas is taken. The proof is rational: waiting for absolute despair would waste the victim's rights, so a practical limit of one year is set because regrowth typically occurs within that time. If after qisas the tooth unexpectedly regrows, the qisas is considered an error due to doubt, and diya (blood money) of five hundred dirhams becomes obligatory. The passage emphasizes that this is an istihsan (juristic preference) to avoid wasting rights.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for a bodily injury, specifically for tooth.
دیت (diyah): Blood money payable in lieu of qisas when qisas cannot be carried out or is dropped.
ارش (arsh): Compensation for a bodily injury that does not have a fixed diyah amount; here used for tooth diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if qisas is taken for a tooth that later regrows?
A) The qisas is void and the victim must return the tooth.
B) No liability because the qisas was already carried out.
C) Diyah (five hundred dirhams) becomes obligatory due to doubt.
D) The aggressor must pay half diyah.

Key Takeaway
A one-year wait is procedural for tooth qisas, but for adults the mufta bihi is no wait; if qisas is taken and the tooth regrows, diyah is obligatory due to doubt.

Dispute Before the Year: Victim's Word Accepted
Ruling on dispute before one year when tooth falls: victim's word is taken

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the cause of a fallen tooth is disputed before the one-year respite and explain the rationale based on istihsan.

Core Idea
The victim's word is accepted to preserve the purpose of the respite.

In diyat (blood money) cases involving tooth injuries, Hanafi law grants a one-year respite to observe the tooth's fate. What happens if the tooth falls before the year ends and the parties dispute the cause? This lesson examines the rule: the victim's statement is taken over the aggressor's. The proof is istihsan—a rational inference that upholds the benefit of the waiting period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Consider the facts: a tooth is struck and becomes loose, a one-year respite is granted, before the year ends the tooth falls, and the parties dispute whether the fall was from the original blow or another cause.
2
Identify the ruling
The text states: 'القول للمضروب' (the victim's word is taken). The victim's assertion is given precedence over the aggressor's denial.
3
Identify the proof and its type
The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The proof is rational—it serves the purpose of the respite.
Istihsan here overrides qiyas, which might require evidence from the victim.
4
Explain the rationale
The purpose of the one-year respite is to allow the effect of the blow to become clear. If the victim's word were not accepted, the aggressor could always claim an alternative cause, rendering the respite useless. Accepting the victim's word makes the respite beneficial.
5
Note the condition and boundary
This rule applies only when the dispute arises before the year ends. After the year, the presumption may shift (the aggressor's word is likely taken, though not stated here).
The condition is 'before the year'—the text explicitly limits the ruling to that time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی کے دانت پر مارا اور وہ ہلنے لگا تو ایک سال مہلت دی جائے گی تا کہ معاملہ کھل کر سامنے آجائے
قال ولو ضرب انسان سن انسان فتحرکت یستأنی حولا ليظهر اثر فعله فلو اجله القاضى سنة ثم جاء المضروب وقد سقطت سنه فاختلفا قبل السنة فيما سقط بضربه فالقول للمضروب ليكون التاجيل مفيدا
ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور اگر کسی شخص نے کسی انسان کے دانت پر مارا پس وہ ملنے لگا تو اس کو ایک سال کی مہلت دی جائے گی تاکہ اس کے فعل کا اثر ظاہر ہو جائے پس اگر قاضی نے اس کو ایک سال کی مہلت دی ہو اور پھر مضروب آیا حالانکہ اس کا دانت گر گیا ہے پس ان دونوں نے ( ضارب اور صاحب دات ینے) اختلاف کیا سال سے پہلے اس چیز کے بارے میں جس کی ضرب سے دانت گرا ہے تو مضروب کا قول معتبر ہوگا تا کہ مہلت دینا مفید ہو۔ تشریح زید نے خالد کے دانت پر چوٹ ماری جس کی وجہ سے وہ ملنے لگا تو اس کو ایک سال کی مہلت دی جائے گی تا کہ زید کے فعل کا اثر اچھی طرح ظاہر ہو جائے۔ پس اگر قاضی نے زید کو ایک سال کی مہلت دے دی اور ابھی سال نہیں گزرا تھا کہ خالد کا دانت گر گیا اور خالد کہتا ہے کہ زید کی چوٹ کی وجہ سے یہ گرا ہے۔ اور زید کہتا ہے کہ نہیں بلکہ میرے بعد خالد کی چوٹ کی وجہ سے گرا ہے تو یہاں مضروب خالد کا قول معتبر ہوگا ورنہ اگر اس کا قول معتبر نہ ہو تو پھر مہلت دینے کا کوئی فائدہ نہ ہوگا۔
If someone strikes another's tooth and it becomes loose, he will be given respite for a year to show the effect of his act. So if the judge gives him a year's respite, then the victim comes and his tooth has fallen, and they dispute before the year about whether it fell from the blow, the victim's statement is accepted so that the respite is beneficial. The commentary explains with an example: Zayd strikes Khalid's tooth, making it loose; a year's respite is given. Before the year ends, the tooth falls. Khalid says it fell from Zayd's blow, Zayd says it fell from another cause. The victim's (Khalid's) word is taken, otherwise the respite would be useless.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah. It addresses a dispute that arises before the one-year respite ends, when the tooth has fallen but the cause is contested. The ruling states that the victim's (the injured person's) statement is accepted over the aggressor's. The proof is rational: the purpose of the respite is to allow time for the effect of the blow to become clear. If the victim's word were not accepted, the aggressor could always claim the fall was from another cause, rendering the respite meaningless. Thus, to preserve the benefit of the waiting period, the victim's assertion is given precedence.

Key Terms
مضروب (madrub): The person struck (the victim).
ضارب (darib): The striker (the aggressor).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is believed if there is a dispute before the one-year period about the cause of the fallen tooth?
A) The aggressor, because he is the defendant.
B) The victim, to make the respite beneficial.
C) Two witnesses.
D) The judge decides by qiyas.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when the cause of a fallen tooth is disputed before the one-year respite, the victim's statement is accepted to uphold the benefit of the waiting period.

Dispute After One Year: Aggressor's Word Accepted
Ruling on burden shift after fixed period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on burden of proof after the one-year period in a diyat case and explain the istihsan reasoning.

Core Idea
After the set term expires, the aggressor's denial is accepted because the default shifts to no causal link.

In the previous lesson, we learned that the judge sets a one-year period for a tooth injury to determine if it falls due to the blow. If a dispute arises after that year about whether the fall was caused by the blow, the ruling is clear: the aggressor's statement is taken. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this shift in burden of proof, based on istihsan.

Guided Path
1
State the Scenario
Identify the key facts: the judge set a one-year period, the period has passed without the tooth falling, and now a dispute arises about the cause.
The dispute is about whether the tooth fell from the original blow or another cause.
2
Identify the Ruling
The text states 'فالقول للضارب' – the word is the aggressor's. That means his denial is accepted without proof.
This is a procedural ruling, not a substantive one about liability.
3
Understand the Proof (Istihsan)
The proof is istihsan: because the fixed term has passed, the default presumption is that the effect (tooth falling) was not from the aggressor's act. Therefore, the denier (aggressor) is believed.
The istihsan overrides the normal rule that the plaintiff must prove causation.
4
Note the Default Presumption
The passage explains 'لانه ينكر اثر فعله وقد مضى الاجل' – he denies the effect of his act and the term has passed. So the legal default returns to innocence.
If the term had not passed, the aggressor would have to prove his innocence.
5
Apply to a Concrete Example
If A strikes B, and B's tooth falls after one year and one day, A denies causation. Since the year has passed, A's word is taken and A owes nothing.
If the tooth had fallen within the year, A would be liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اختلفا في ذالك بعد السنة فالقول للضارب لانه ينكر اثر فعله وقد مضى الاجل الذى وقته القاضي لظهور الاثر فكان القول للمنكر ولو لم تسقط لاشيئ على الضارب
ترجمہ اور اگر ان دونوں نے اختلاف کیا سال کے بعد تو ضارب کا قول معتبر ہوگا اس لئے کہ وہ اپنے فعل کے اثر کا انکار کرتا ہے اور وہ مدت گزر چکی جس کو قاضی نے اثر ظاہر کرنے کے لئے مقرر کیا تھا تو منکر کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور اگر دانت نہ گرتا تو ضارب پر کچھ نہ تھا۔
And if they dispute after the year, the statement is the aggressor's, because he denies the effect of his act, and the term that the judge set for the appearance of the effect has passed, so the denier's statement is taken. And if the tooth did not fall, nothing is owed by the aggressor.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is the concluding ruling. After the one-year respite has passed without the tooth falling, if a dispute arises later about whether the tooth fell from the original blow or another cause, the aggressor's statement is accepted. The proof: the period fixed by the judge for the effect to become apparent has ended, and the aggressor denies that his act caused the fall. Since the term is over, the default is that no effect occurred, so the aggressor's denial is credible. If the tooth never fell, the aggressor owes nothing.

Key Terms
منکر (munkar): The one who denies (here, the aggressor).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After the one-year period, if a dispute arises about the cause of the fallen tooth, who is believed?
A) The victim, because he was injured.
B) The aggressor, because the set period has passed.
C) Two witnesses.
D) The judge decides by qiyas.

Key Takeaway
After the judge's fixed period expires, the aggressor's word is taken due to istihsan, shifting the burden of proof to the victim.

Dispute Before Year Ends for a Loose Tooth in Diyat
Explains who is believed when dispute occurs before one-year respite ends for a loosened tooth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is accepted when a dispute arises before the one-year respite ends for a loosened tooth, and explain the legal reasoning (istihsan) behind it.

Core Idea
The victim's claim is accepted to preserve the benefit of the one-year respite.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Kitab al-Diyat concerning injuries to teeth. When a tooth becomes loose due to a blow, the striker is given a one-year waiting period to observe the final outcome. But what happens if the victim claims the tooth fell from that blow before the year ends, while the striker denies? The Hanafi school has a clear position based on istihsan, which we will explore.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
There is a striker (ḍārib) who strikes a victim (maḍrūb) causing a tooth to become loose. The judge grants a one-year respite (muhlat) to observe the effect of the injury.
The respite is a standard Hanafi rule for certain injuries.
2
State the point of dispute
Before the year ends, the tooth falls. The victim claims it fell due to the original blow; the striker claims it fell due to a later cause (e.g., another blow or accident).
The dispute is about causation.
3
State the Hanafi ruling
The victim's statement (qawl al-maḍrūb) is accepted, and the striker is liable for the full diyat of a tooth.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Analyze the proof: istihsan
The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to ensure the respite serves its purpose. If the victim's statement were not accepted, the respite would be meaningless, as the striker could always deny causation.
The alternative (qiyas) might require the victim to bring evidence, but istihsan overrides that.
5
Consider the consequence
If the victim's statement were rejected, the striker could escape liability by simply denying, and the purpose of the respite—to observe the full effect—would be defeated.
This illustrates the principle that legal rulings are designed to achieve practical benefits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور اگر کسی شخص نے کسی انسان کے دانت پر مارا پس وہ ملنے لگا تو اس کو ایک سال کی مہلت دی جائے گی تاکہ اس کے فعل کا اثر ظاہر ہو جائے پس اگر قاضی نے اس کو ایک سال کی مہلت دی ہو اور پھر مضروب آیا حالانکہ اس کا دانت گر گیا ہے پس ان دونوں نے ( ضارب اور صاحب دا زینے) اختلاف کیا سال سے پہلے اس چیز کے بارے میں جس کی ضرب سے دانت گرا ہے تو مضروب کا قول معتبر ہوگا تا کہ مہلت دینا مفید ہو۔ تشریح زید نے خالد کے دانت پر چوٹ ماری جس کی وجہ سے وہ ملنے لگا تو اس کو ایک سال کی مہلت دی جائے گی تا کہ زید کے فعل کا اثر اچھی طرح ظاہر ہو جائے۔ پس اگر قاضی نے زید کو ایک سال کی مہلت دے دی اور ابھی سال نہیں گزرا تھا کہ خالد کا دانت گر گیا اور خالد کہتا ہے کہ زید کی چوٹ کی وجہ سے یہ گرا ہے اور زید کہتا ہے کہ نہیں بلکہ میرے بعد خالد کی چوٹ کی وجہ سے گرا ہے تو یہاں مضروب خالد کا قول معتبر ہوگا ورنہ اگر اس کا قول معتبر نہ ہو تو پھر مہلت دینے کا کوئی فائدہ نہ ہوگا۔
The author said: And if someone strikes a person's tooth and it becomes loose, then he will be given a respite of one year so that the effect of his action becomes apparent. Then if the judge gave him a respite of one year and the victim came while his tooth has fallen, and the two (the striker and the owner of the tooth) disputed about the thing by whose blow the tooth fell before the year, then the statement of the victim will be accepted so that the respite is beneficial. Commentary: Zaid struck Khalid's tooth, causing it to become loose. So a one-year respite will be given so that the effect of Zaid's action becomes clear. Then if the judge gave Zaid a respite of one year and before the year passed Khalid's tooth fell, and Khalid says it fell due to Zaid's blow, and Zaid says no, but rather it fell due to a blow after mine, then here the statement of the victim Khalid will be accepted; otherwise, if his statement is not accepted, then there would be no benefit in giving the respite.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage deals with a ruling from Kitab al-Diyat concerning injuries to teeth. The Hanafi position: when a tooth becomes loose due to a blow, the striker is given a one-year respite to see if the tooth falls. If a dispute arises before the year ends about whether the fall was caused by the original blow or a subsequent event, the victim's claim that it fell from the original blow is accepted. The reasoning is istihsan: to preserve the benefit of the respite, as otherwise the respite would be pointless. This is based on the principle that apparent causation and presumptive evidence are considered. The commentary illustrates with Zaid and Khalid.

Key Terms
ضارب (ḍārib): The person who strikes the blow.
مضروب (maḍrūb): The person who is struck, the victim.
مہلت (muhlat): A respite or waiting period, here one year, to observe the effect of the injury.
قول معتبر (qawl muʿtabar): The statement that is accepted and given legal weight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The victim's statement
C) The judge's decision
D) A medical expert's opinion

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a dispute arises before the one-year respite ends for a loosened tooth, the victim's statement is accepted to ensure the respite serves its purpose.

Distinction: Shajjah Munqilah vs. Tooth Injury
Contrasts rulings for head wound vs. tooth injury

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is accepted when a head wound progresses from mudihah to munqilah, and distinguish it from the tooth injury case by identifying the underlying causal reasoning.

Core Idea
A mudihah does not naturally become a munqilah; shaking a tooth does cause its fall. The striker's denial is accepted in the former, the victim's claim in the latter.

This passage expands a legal principle by contrasting two injury scenarios: a head wound that becomes a displaced fracture and a tooth that is shaken loose. The distinction turns on the apparent causal connection between the initial act and the eventual harm. Understanding why the rulings differ sharpens your ability to apply istihsan in diyat cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario A: a head wound initially a mudihah (exposing bone) later becomes a munqilah (displaced fracture). Scenario B: a tooth is shaken and falls out. Note the procedural dispute: in A, the striker denies causing the worsening; in B, the victim claims the shaking caused the fall.
2
State the ruling in each scenario
In Scenario A, the striker's statement is accepted (he is believed that his blow did not cause the munqilah). In Scenario B, the victim's claim is accepted that the shaking caused the fall.
3
Extract the rationale (dalil)
The rationale for A: a mudihah does not typically cause a munqilah; another cause is likely. For B: shaking a tooth directly causes its fall. The apparent evidence (zahir al-hal) supports the striker in A and the victim in B.
This is an application of istihsan based on customary causation (urf).
4
Understand the qiyas khafi dimension
The tooth case is the default (ash: where the act is likely to produce the harm). The head wound case is distinguished because mudihah does not produce munqilah. The subtle analogy (qiyas khafi) is that the ruling follows the likelihood of causation.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Test similar scenarios: e.g., a wound that is a simha (crack to the bone but no displacement) later becomes a munqilah. Would the ruling be the same? The principle: if the original wound type does not usually cause the worsened condition, the striker is believed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا بخلاف ما اذا شجه موضحة فجاء وقد صارت منقلة فاختلفا حيث يكون القول قول الضارب لان الموضحة لاتورث المنقلة اما التحريك فيؤثر فى السقوط فافترقا ترجمہ اور یہ اس صورت کے خلاف ہے جب کہ اس کو شجہ موضحہ پہنچایا ہو تو وہ ( مشجو ج) آیا حالانکہ وہ منقلہ ہو گیا پس ان دونوں نے اختلاف کیا تو قول ضارب کا معتبر ہو گا اس لئے کہ موضحہ منتقلہ کو پیدا نہیں کرتا، بہر حال دانت کو ہلا دینا گرنے میں مؤثر ہے تو یہ دونوں الگ الگ ہو گئے ۔ تشریح زید نے عمرد کے سر پر مار کر اس کو زخمی کر دیا اور وہ زخم ہو گیا جس کو موضحہ کہتے ہیں پھر اس کا منقلہ ہو گیا اور اب زید اور عمرو میں اختلاف ہوا، عمر کہتا ہے کہ اس کا منقلہ ہونا تیرے زخم کی وجہ سے ہے اور زید کہتا ہے کہ نہیں بلکہ خالد کے مارنے کی وجہ سے ہے تو یہاں شاج یعنی زید کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ یہاں ظاہر حال زید کے لئے شاہد ہے کیونکہ موضحہ منتقلہ بننے کا باعث نہیں ہے اور دانت کو ہلا دینا سقوط کا باعث بنتا ہے۔
This is contrary to the case where he inflicted a mudihah wound on him, and he came and it had become a munqilah, and they disputed; then the statement of the striker is accepted, because a mudihah does not cause a munqilah. However, shaking the tooth affects its fall, so they differ. Commentary: Zaid hit Amr's head and wounded him, and the wound became a mudihah, then it became a munqilah. Now Zaid and Amr disputed: Amr says it became munqilah due to your wound, and Zaid says due to Khalid's hitting. Here the statement of the striker, Zaid, is accepted because apparent evidence supports him, as a mudihah does not cause a munqilah, while shaking a tooth causes its fall.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage compares two scenarios. In the case of a head wound that is initially a mudihah (a wound exposing the bone) and later becomes a munqilah (a wound that displaces the bone), the striker's denial that his blow caused the worsening is accepted. The reason is that a mudihah by itself does not ordinarily turn into a munqilah; a separate cause is likely. In contrast, shaking a tooth can directly cause its fall, so in the tooth case the victim's claim is accepted. This distinction is based on the apparent nature of the injuries, a form of istihsan.

Key Terms
موضحہ (mūḍiḥah): A head wound that exposes the bone but does not fracture it.
منقلہ (munqilah): A head wound that displaces the bone (fracture with displacement).
شجہ (shajjah): A head wound; general term for wounds on the head.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The ruling on whose statement is accepted in a diyat dispute depends on whether the initial injury is a customary cause of the worsened condition.

Dispute After the Year: Striker Is Believed
States that if dispute occurs after the one-year respite, the striker's denial is accepted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that after the one-year respite, the striker's denial is accepted and explain the rationale based on the passing of the observation period.

Core Idea
After the one-year respite, the striker's denial is accepted because the time for observing the effect has passed.

This lesson addresses a specific procedural rule in Hanafi diyat for tooth injuries. When a dispute arises between the striker and the victim after the one-year respite period, the striker's denial regarding the cause of the tooth's fall is credited. This rule is grounded in the purpose of the respite and the general principle that the denier's statement is accepted in the absence of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Dispute
The act is the striker hitting the victim, causing a tooth injury. The dispute is about whether the tooth fell due to the striker's action.
The tooth injury is specific; the rule applies only after the one-year respite.
2
Check the Time Condition
The dispute occurs after the one-year respite set by the judge. This is the key procedural condition.
If within the year, the rule differs.
3
Apply the Rule
The striker's denial is accepted. The striker is believed because he denies the effect of his act.
The rule is stated as al-qawl li-l-ḍārib.
4
Understand the Rationale
The purpose of the respite was to allow observation of the effect; after it passes, the striker becomes a denier and his statement is credited.
This shifts the presumption from the striker to the victim after the period.
5
Note the Subsidiary Ruling
If the tooth never falls even after the respite, nothing is obligatory upon the striker.
This resolves any remaining doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اختلفا في ذالك بعد السنة فالقول للضارب لانه ينكر اثر فعله وقد مضى الاجل الذى وقته القاضي ـظهور الأثر فكان القول للمنكر ولو لم تسقط لاشيئ على الضارب ترجمہ اور اگر ان دونوں نے اختلاف کیا سال کے تو ضارب کا قول معتبر ہوگا اس لئے کہ وہ اپنے فعل کے اثر کا انکار کرتا ہے اور وہ مدت گذر چکی ہے جس کو قاضی نے ظہور اثر کے لئے مقرر کیا تھا تو منکر کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور اگر دانت نہ گرا ہو تو ضارب پر کچھ نہیں ہے۔ تشریح اگر ضارب و مضروب ( زید و عمرو) کا اختلاف مذکور مدت مقررہ گذرنے کے بعد ہو تو پھر مضارب کا قول معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ یہاں مضروب مدعی ہے کہ دانت ضارب کے فعل کے اثر سے گرا ہے اور ضارب اپنے فعل کے اثر سے گرنے کا انکار کرتا ہے تو جہاں منکر کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ اختلاف مدت گذرنے کے بعد ہے۔ اور مدت گزرگئی اور دانت نہیں گرا تو پھر ضارب پر کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا۔
And if they dispute about that after the year, then the statement is of the striker, because he denies the effect of his action, and the term that the judge set for appearance of effect has passed, so the statement is of the denier. And if it did not fall, then nothing is upon the striker. Commentary: If the dispute between striker and victim occurs after the specified period, then the striker's statement is accepted because here the victim claims the fall was due to the striker's act, and the striker denies that it fell due to his act. So the denier's statement is accepted. And if the period passed and the tooth did not fall, then nothing is obligatory upon the striker.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This lesson presents a clear procedural rule: once the one-year respite has passed without the tooth falling, or if a dispute arises after that period, the striker's denial is credited. The reasoning is that the purpose of the respite was to observe the effect; after that time, the presumption shifts to the striker. If the tooth never falls, no diyat is due. This is consistent with the general principle that the defendant's denial is accepted if there is no contrary evidence.

Key Terms
السنة (al-sanah): The one-year respite period for observing the effect of the injury.
القول للضارب (al-qawl li-l-ḍārib): The statement (of the striker) is accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The victim
B) The striker
C) A medical expert
D) The judge

Key Takeaway
After the one-year respite, the striker's denial is accepted, and if the tooth never falls, nothing is due.
